ADVERTISEMENT.

JVT  O S T of  the  principles,  and  reafonings,  con-
tained in  this  volume,  were  publiflied  in  a work  in
three  volumes,  called  A 'Treatije  of  Human  Nature  t
A work  which  the  Author  had  projefted  before  he
left  College,  and  which  he  wrote  and  publiflied
not  long  after.  But  not  finding  it  fuccefsful,  he
was  fenfible  of  his  error  in  going  to  the  prefs  too
early,  and  he  caft  the  whole  anew  in  the  following
pieces,  where  fome  negligences  in  his  former  rea-
ibning,  and  more  in  the  expreflion,  are,  he  hopes,
correfted.  Yet  feveral  writers,  who  have  honoured
the  Author’s  Philofophy  with  anfwers,  have  taken
care  to  diredi:  all  their  batteries  againfl:  that  juve-
nile work,  which  the  Author  never  acknowledged,
and  have,  affected  to  triumph  in  any  advantages,
which,  they  imagined,  they  had  obtained  over  it  :
A pradtice  very  contrary  to  all  rules  of  candour  and
fair-dealing,  and  a ftrong  inftance  of  thofe  pole-
mical artifices,  which  a bigotted  zeal  thinks  itfelf
authorifed  to  employ.  Henceforth,  the  Author
dcfires,  that  the  following  Pieces  may  alone  be  re-
garded as  containing  his  philofophical  fentimenta
and  principles.

Of  the  Different  Species  of  Philosophy.

M ORAL  philofophy,  or  the  fcience  of  human
nature,  may  be  treated  after  two  different  man-
ners ; each  of  which  has  its  peculiar  merit,  and
may  contribute  to  the  entertainment,  inftruftion,
and  reformation  of  mankind.  The  one  confiders
man  chiefly  as  born  for  action  ; and  as  influenced
in  his  meafures  by  tafte  and  fentiment ; purfuing
one  objedt,  and  avoiding  another,  according  to  the
value  which  thefe  objefls  feem  to  poffefs,  and  ac-
cording to  the  light  in  which  they  prefent  them-
felves.  As  virtue,  of  all  objedls,  is  allowed  to  be
the  moft  valuable,  this  fpecies  of  philofophers  paint
her  in  the  moft  amiable  colours  j borrowing  all
helps  from  poetry  and  eloquence,  and  treating
their  fubjeft  in  an  eafy  and  obvious  manner,  and
fuch  as  is  beft  fitted  to  pleafe  the  imagination,
and  engage  the  affedtions.  They  feleft  the  moft
ftriking  obfervations  and  inftances  frcm  common
life;  place  oppofite  charafters  in  a proper  contraft;
and  alluring  us  im.^  the  paths  of  virtue  by  the  views
of  glory  and  happinefs,  diredt  our  fteps  in  thefe
paths  by  the  foundeft  precepts  and  moft  illuftrious
.examples.  They  make  us  feel  the  difference  be-
tween vice  and  virtue ; they  excite  and  regulate
our  fentiments;  and  fo  they  can  but  bend  our  hearts

to  the  love  of  probity  and  true  honour,  they  think,

The  other  fpecies  of  philofophers  confider  man
in  the  light  of  a reafonable  rather  than  an  aftive

Though  their  fpeculations  feem  abftrac^,  and  even

It  is  certain  that  the  eafy  a|).d  obvious  philo-
fophy, will  always,  with  the  generality  of  man-
kind, have  the  preference  above  the  accurate  and
abftrufe ; and  by  many  will  be  recommended,

than  the  other.  It  enters  more  into  common

P A R T II.

Nothing  is  more,  free  than  the  imagination  of
man  ; and  though  it  cannot  exceed  that  original
flock  of  ideas,  furnifhed  by  the  internal  and  ex-
ternal fenfes,  it  has  unlimited  power  of  mixing,

itfelf  with  every  circumftance,  that  belongs  to  any
hiftorical  fa6t,  which  it  believes  with  the  greateft
certainty.  Wherein,  therefore,  confifts  the  differ-

merely  in  any  peculiar  idea,  which  is  annexed  to
fuch  a conception  as  commands  our  affent,  arj^

p S E C T I O N V.

which  is  wanting  to  every  known  fi£lion.  _ For  as
the  mind  has  authority  over  all  its  ideas,  it  could

Of  the  Reason  of  Animals.

Al  L our  reafonings  concerning  matter  of  fafc
are  founded  on  a fpecies  of  Analogy,  which  leads

jedls  have  not  fo  exadf  a fimilarity,  the  analogy  is

though  ftill  it  has  fome  force,  in  proportion  to  the
